### Degree Requirements for 6-P4\_2025    MNG in Artificial Intelligence and Decision Making

Show old EECS subject numbers

_Required subjects:_

-
One **programming skills** subject:

| [6.1000\<br>**6.1000 Introduction to Programming and Computer Science**\<br>\<br>* * *\<br>\<br>![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/under.gif) (![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/fall.gif),![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/spring.gif))\<br>\<br>Prereqs: none\<br>\<br>Units: 4-0-8\<br>\<br>* * *\<br>\<br>Develops foundational skills in programming and in computational modeling. Covers widely used programming concepts in Python, including mutability, function objects, and object-oriented programming. Introduces algorithmic complexity and some common libraries. Throughout, demonstrates using computation to help understand real-world phenomena; topics include optimization problems, building simulations, and statistical modeling. Intended for students with at least some prior exposure to programming. Students with no programming experience are encouraged to take 6.100A and 6.100B (or 16.C20) over two terms.](http://student.mit.edu/catalog/search.cgi?search=6.1000)<br>[6.100A6.0001\<br>**6.100A Introduction to Computer Science Programming in Python**\<br>\<br>* * *\<br>\<br>![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/under.gif) (![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/fall.gif),![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/spring.gif))\<br>\<br>Prereqs: none\<br>\<br>Units: 2-0-4\<br>\<br>* * *\<br>\<br>Introduction to computer science and programming. Students develop skills to program and use computational techniques to solve problems. Topics include: the notion of computation, Python, simple algorithms and data structures, object-oriented programming, testing and debugging, and algorithmic complexity. Lectures are viewed outside of class; in-class time is dedicated to problem-solving and discussion. Combination of 6.100A and 6.100B (or 16.C20) counts as REST subject.](http://student.mit.edu/catalog/search.cgi?search=6.100A)& [16.C20\<br>**16.C20 Introduction to Computational Science and Engineering**\<br>\<br>* * *\<br>\<br>![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/under.gif) (![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/fall.gif),![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/spring.gif))\<br>\<br>(Same subject as 9.C20, 18.C20, CSE.C20)\<br>\<br>Prereqs/\[Coreqs\]: 6.100A; \[8.01 and 18.01\]\<br>\<br>Units: 2-0-4\<br>\<br>* * *\<br>\<br>Provides an introduction to computational algorithms used throughout engineering and science (natural and social) to simulate time-dependent phenomena; optimize and control systems; and quantify uncertainty in problems involving randomness, including an introduction to probability and statistics. -
Three **math** subjects:

[6.12006.042\\
**6.1200 Mathematics for Computer Science**\\
\\
* * *\\
\\
![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/under.gif) (![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/fall.gif),![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/spring.gif))\\
\\
(Same subject as 18.062)\\
\\
Prereqs: GIR:CAL1\\
\\
Units: 5-0-7\\
\\
* * *\\
\\
Elementary discrete mathematics for science and engineering, with a focus on mathematical tools and proof techniques useful in computer science. Topics include logical notation, sets, relations, elementary graph theory, state machines and invariants, induction and proofs by contradiction, recurrences, asymptotic notation, elementary analysis of algorithms, elementary number theory and cryptography, permutations and combinations, counting tools, and discrete probability.](http://student.mit.edu/catalog/search.cgi?search=6.1200)

One of
[18.06![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/spring.gif)\\
**18.06 Linear Algebra**\\
\\
* * *\\
\\
![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/under.gif) (![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/spring.gif))\\
\\
Prereqs: GIR:CAL2\\
\\
Units: 4-0-8\\
\\
* * *\\
\\
Basic subject on matrix theory and linear algebra, emphasizing topics useful in other disciplines, including systems of equations, vector spaces, determinants, eigenvalues, singular value decomposition, and positive definite matrices. Applications to least-squares approximations, stability of differential equations, networks, Fourier transforms, and Markov processes. Uses linear algebra software. Compared with 18.700, more emphasis on matrix algorithms and many applications.](http://student.mit.edu/catalog/search.cgi?search=18.06), [18.C06![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/fall.gif)\\
**18.C06 Linear Algebra and Optimization**\\
\\
* * *\\
\\
![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/under.gif) (![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/fall.gif))\\
\\
(Same subject as 6.C06)\\
\\
Prereqs: GIR:CAL2\\
\\
Units: 5-0-7\\
\\
* * *\\
\\
Introductory course in linear algebra and optimization, assuming no prior exposure to linear algebra and starting from the basics, including vectors, matrices, eigenvalues, singular values, and least squares. Covers the basics in optimization including convex optimization, linear/quadratic programming, gradient descent, and regularization, building on insights from linear algebra. Explores a variety of applications in science and engineering, where the tools developed give powerful ways to understand complex systems and also extract structure from data.](http://student.mit.edu/catalog/search.cgi?search=18.C06), [6.C06![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/fall.gif)\\
**6.C06 Linear Algebra and Optimization**\\
\\
* * *\\
\\
![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/under.gif) (![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/fall.gif))\\
\\
(Same subject as 18.C06)\\
\\
Prereqs: GIR:CAL2\\
\\
Units: 5-0-7\\
\\
* * *\\
\\
Introductory course in linear algebra and optimization, assuming no prior exposure to linear algebra and starting from the basics, including vectors, matrices, eigenvalues, singular values, and least squares. Covers the basics in optimization including convex optimization, linear/quadratic programming, gradient descent, and regularization, building on insights from linear algebra. Explores a variety of applications in science and engineering, where the tools developed give powerful ways to understand complex systems and also extract structure from data.](http://student.mit.edu/catalog/search.cgi?search=6.C06)

One of
[18.05![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/spring.gif)\\
**18.05 Introduction to Probability and Statistics**\\
\\
* * *\\
\\
![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/under.gif) (![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/spring.gif))\\
\\
Prereqs: GIR:CAL2\\
\\
Units: 4-0-8\\
\\
* * *\\
\\
A unified introduction to probability, Bayesian inference, and frequentist statistics. Topics include: combinatorics, random variables, (joint) distributions, covariance, central limit theorem; Bayesian updating, odds, posterior prediction; significance tests, confidence intervals, bootstrapping, regression. Students also develop computational skills and statistical thinking by using R to simulate, analyze, and visualize data; and by exploring privacy, fairness, and causality in contemporary media and research. Flipped subject taught in a Technology Enabled Active Learning (TEAL) classroom to facilitate discussion, group problem solving, and coding studios with ample mentorship.](http://student.mit.edu/catalog/search.cgi?search=18.05), [6.37006.041\\
**6.3700 Introduction to Probability**\\
\\
* * *\\
\\
![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/under.gif) (![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/fall.gif),![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/spring.gif))\\
\\
Prereqs: GIR:CAL2\\
\\
Units: 4-0-8\\
\\
* * *\\
\\
An introduction to probability theory, the modeling and analysis of probabilistic systems, and elements of statistical inference. Probabilistic models, conditional probability. Discrete and continuous random variables. Expectation and conditional expectation, and further topics about random variables. Limit Theorems. Bayesian estimation and hypothesis testing. Elements of classical statistical inference. Bernoulli and Poisson processes. Markov chains. Students taking graduate version complete additional assignments.](http://student.mit.edu/catalog/search.cgi?search=6.3700), [6.38006.008![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/fall.gif)\\
**6.3800 Introduction to Inference**\\
\\
* * *\\
\\
![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/under.gif) (![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/fall.gif))\\
\\
Prereqs: GIR:CAL2 or permission of instructor\\
\\
Units: 4-4-4\\
\\
* * *\\
\\
Introduces probabilistic modeling for problems of inference and machine learning from data, emphasizing analytical and computational aspects. Distributions, marginalization, conditioning, and structure, including graphical and neural network representations. Belief propagation, decision-making, classification, estimation, and prediction. Sampling methods and analysis. Introduces asymptotic analysis and information measures. Computational laboratory component explores the concepts introduced in class in the context of contemporary applications. Students design inference algorithms, investigate their behavior on real data, and discuss experimental results.](http://student.mit.edu/catalog/search.cgi?search=6.3800)

-
Two **foundation** subjects:

[6.10106.009\\
**6.1010 Fundamentals of Programming**\\
\\
* * *\\
\\
![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/under.gif) (![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/fall.gif),![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/spring.gif))\\
\\
Prereqs: 6.1000 or (6.100A and (6.100B or 16.C20))\\
\\
Units: 2-4-6\\
\\
* * *\\
\\
Introduces fundamental concepts of programming. Designed to develop skills in applying basic methods from programming languages to abstract problems. Topics include programming and Python basics, computational concepts, software engineering, algorithmic techniques, data types, and recursion. Lab component consists of software design, construction, and implementation of design. Enrollment may be limited.](http://student.mit.edu/catalog/search.cgi?search=6.1010)

[6.12106.006\\
**6.1210 Introduction to Algorithms**\\
\\
* * *\\
\\
![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/under.gif) (![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/fall.gif),![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/spring.gif))\\
\\
Prereqs: 6.100A and (6.1200 or (6.120A and (6.3700, 6.3800, 18.05, or 18.600)))\\
\\
Units: 5-0-7\\
\\
* * *\\
\\
Introduction to mathematical modeling of computational problems, as well as common algorithms, algorithmic paradigms, and data structures used to solve these problems. Emphasizes the relationship between algorithms and programming, and introduces basic performance measures and analysis techniques for these problems. Enrollment may be limited.](http://student.mit.edu/catalog/search.cgi?search=6.1210)

-
Five **Center** subjects:

| [6.12206.046\<br>**6.1220 Design and Analysis of Algorithms**\<br>\<br>* * *\<br>\<br>![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/under.gif) (![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/fall.gif),![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/spring.gif))\<br>\<br>(Same subject as 18.410)\<br>\<br>Prereqs: 6.1200 and 6.1210\<br>\<br>Units: 4-0-8\<br>\<br>* * *\<br>\<br>Techniques for the design and analysis of efficient algorithms, emphasizing methods useful in practice. Topics include sorting; search trees, heaps, and hashing; divide-and-conquer; dynamic programming; greedy algorithms; amortized analysis; graph algorithms; and shortest paths. Advanced topics may include network flow; computational geometry; number-theoretic algorithms; polynomial and matrix calculations; caching; and parallel computing.](http://student.mit.edu/catalog/search.cgi?search=6.1220)<br>[6.14006.045![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/spring.gif)\<br>**6.1400 Computability and Complexity Theory**\<br>\<br>* * *\<br>\<br>![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/under.gif) (![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/spring.gif))\<br>\<br>(Same subject as 18.400)\<br>\<br>Prereqs: (6.1200 and 6.1210) or permission of instructor\<br>\<br>Units: 4-0-8\<br>\<br>* * *\<br>\<br>Mathematical introduction to the theory of computing. Rigorously explores what kinds of tasks can be efficiently solved with computers by way of finite automata, circuits, Turing machines, and communication complexity, introducing students to some major open problems in mathematics. Builds skills in classifying computational tasks in terms of their difficulty. Discusses other fundamental issues in computing, including the Halting Problem, the Church-Turing Thesis, the P versus NP problem, and the power of randomness.](http://student.mit.edu/catalog/search.cgi?search=6.1400)<br>[6.30006.003\<br>**6.3000 Signal Processing**\<br>\<br>* * *\<br>\<br>![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/under.gif) (![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/fall.gif),![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/spring.gif))\<br>\<br>Prereqs: 6.100A\<br>\<br>Units: 4-0-8\<br>\<br>* * *\<br>\<br>Fundamentals of signal processing, focusing on the use of Fourier methods to analyze and process signals such as sounds and images. Topics include Fourier series, Fourier transforms, the Discrete Fourier Transform, sampling, convolution, deconvolution, filtering, noise reduction, and compression. Applications draw broadly from areas of contemporary interest with emphasis on both analysis and design.](http://student.mit.edu/catalog/search.cgi?search=6.3000)<br>[6.31006.302\<br>**6.3100 Dynamical System Modeling and Control Design**\<br>\<br>* * *\<br>\<br>![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/under.gif) (![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/fall.gif),![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/spring.gif))\<br>\<br>Prereqs: GIR:PHY2 and (18.06 or 18.C06)\<br>\<br>Units: 4-4-4\<br>\<br>* * *\<br>\<br>A learn-by-design introduction to modeling and control of discrete- and continuous-time systems, from intuition-building analytical techniques to more computational and data-centric strategies. Topics include: linear difference/differential equations (natural frequencies, transfer functions); controller metrics (stability, tracking, disturbance rejection); analytical techniques (PID, root-loci, lead-lag, phase margin); computational strategies (state-space, eigen-placement, LQR); and data-centric approaches (state estimation, regression, and identification). Concepts are introduced with lectures and online problems, and then mastered during weekly labs. In lab, students model, design, test, and explain systems and controllers involving sensors, actuators, and a microcontroller (e.g., optimizing thrust-driven positioners or stabilizing magnetic levitators). Students taking graduate version complete additional problems and labs.](http://student.mit.edu/catalog/search.cgi?search=6.3100)<br>[6.32606.207![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/fall.gif)\<br>**6.3260 Networks**\<br>\<br>* * *\<br>\<br>![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/under.gif) (![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/fall.gif))\<br>\<br>(Same subject as 14.15)\<br>\<br>Prereqs: 6.3700 or 14.30\<br>\<br>Units: 4-0-8\<br>\<br>* * *\<br>\<br>Highlights common principles that permeate the functioning of diverse technological, economic and social networks. Utilizes three sets of tools for analyzing networks -- random graph models, optimization, and game theory -- to study informational and learning cascades; economic and financial networks; social influence networks; formation of social groups; communication networks and the Internet; consensus and gossiping; spread and control of epidemics; control and use of energy networks; and biological networks. Students taking graduate version complete additional assignments.](http://student.mit.edu/catalog/search.cgi?search=6.3260) | [6.37206.401![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/spring.gif)\<br>**6.3720 Introduction to Statistical Data Analysis**\<br>\<br>* * *\<br>\<br>![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/under.gif) (![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/spring.gif))\<br>\<br>Prereqs: 6.100A and (6.3700, 6.3800, or 18.600)\<br>\<br>Units: 4-0-8\<br>\<br>* * *\<br>\<br>Introduction to the central concepts and methods of data science with an emphasis on statistical grounding and modern computational capabilities. Covers principles involved in extracting information from data for the purpose of making predictions or decisions, including data exploration, feature selection, model fitting, and performance assessment. Topics include learning of distributions, hypothesis testing (including multiple comparison procedures), linear and nonlinear regression and prediction, classification, time series, uncertainty quantification, model validation, causal inference, optimization, and decisions. Computational case studies and projects drawn from applications in finance, sports, engineering, and machine learning life sciences. Students taking graduate version complete additional assignments. Recommended prerequisite: 18.06.](http://student.mit.edu/catalog/search.cgi?search=6.3720)<br>[6.39006.036\<br>**6.3900 Introduction to Machine Learning**\<br>\<br>* * *\<br>\<br>![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/under.gif) (![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/fall.gif),![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/spring.gif))\<br>\<br>Prereqs: (6.1000 or 6.1210) and (18.03 or 18.06)\<br>\<br>Units: 4-0-8\<br>\<br>* * *\<br>\<br>Introduction to the principles and algorithms of machine learning from an optimization perspective. Topics include linear and non-linear models for supervised, unsupervised, and reinforcement learning, with a focus on gradient-based methods and neural-network architectures. Previous experience with algorithms may be helpful.](http://student.mit.edu/catalog/search.cgi?search=6.3900)<br>[6.39506.404![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/fall.gif)\<br>**6.3950 AI, Decision Making, and Society**\<br>\<br>* * *\<br>\<br>![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/under.gif) (![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/fall.gif))\<br>\<br>Prereqs/\[Coreqs\]: \[6.1200, 6.3700, 6.3800, 18.05, or 18.600\]\<br>\<br>Units: 4-0-8\<br>\<br>* * *\<br>\<br>Introduction to fundamentals of modern data-driven decision-making frameworks, such as causal inference and hypothesis testing in statistics as well as supervised and reinforcement learning in machine learning. Explores how these frameworks are being applied in various societal contexts, including criminal justice, healthcare, finance, and social media. Emphasis on pinpointing the non-obvious interactions, undesirable feedback loops, and unintended consequences that arise in such settings. Enables students to develop their own principled perspective on the interface of data-driven decision making and society. Students taking graduate version complete additional assignments.](http://student.mit.edu/catalog/search.cgi?search=6.3950)<br>[6.41106.038![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/spring.gif)\<br>**6.4110 Representation, Inference, and Reasoning in AI**\<br>\<br>* * *\<br>\<br>![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/under.gif) (![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/spring.gif))\<br>\<br>Prereqs: 6.1010, 6.1210, and (6.3700, 6.3800, 18.05, or 18.600)\<br>\<br>Units: 3-0-9\<br>\<br>* * *\<br>\<br>An introduction to representations and algorithms for artificial intelligence. Topics covered include: constraint satisfaction in discrete and continuous problems, logical representation and inference, Monte Carlo tree search, probabilistic graphical models and inference, planning in discrete and continuous deterministic and probabilistic models including Markov decision processes (MDPs) and partially observed Markov decision processes (POMDPs).](http://student.mit.edu/catalog/search.cgi?search=6.4110)<br>[6.41206.804![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/fall.gif)\<br>**6.4120 Computational Cognitive Science**\<br>\<br>* * *\<br>\<br>![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/under.gif) (![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/fall.gif))\<br>\<br>(Same subject as 9.66)\<br>\<br>Prereqs: 6.3700, 6.3800, 9.40, 18.05, 6.3900, or permission of instructor\<br>\<br>Units: 3-0-9\<br>\<br>* * *\<br>\<br>Introduction to computational theories of human cognition. Focus on principles of inductive learning and inference, and the representation of knowledge. Computational frameworks covered include Bayesian and hierarchical Bayesian models; probabilistic graphical models; nonparametric statistical models and the Bayesian Occam's razor; sampling algorithms for approximate learning and inference; and probabilistic models defined over structured representations such as first-order logic, grammars, or relational schemas. Applications to understanding core aspects of cognition, such as concept learning and categorization, causal reasoning, theory formation, language acquisition, and social inference. Graduate students complete a final project.](http://student.mit.edu/catalog/search.cgi?search=6.4120) | [6.44006.837![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/fall.gif)\<br>**6.4400 Computer Graphics**\<br>\<br>* * *\<br>\<br>![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/under.gif) (![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/fall.gif))\<br>\<br>Prereqs: 6.1010 and (18.06 or 18.C06)\<br>\<br>Units: 3-0-9\<br>\<br>* * *\<br>\<br>Introduction to computer graphics algorithms, software and hardware. Topics include ray tracing, the graphics pipeline, transformations, texture mapping, shadows, sampling, global illumination, splines, animation and color.](http://student.mit.edu/catalog/search.cgi?search=6.4400)<br>[6.44206.807![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/fall.gif)\<br>**6.4420 Computational Design and Fabrication**\<br>\<br>* * *\<br>\<br>![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/under.gif) (![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/fall.gif))\<br>\<br>(Same subject as 2.0911)\<br>\<br>Prereqs: GIR:CAL2 and (6.1010 or permission of instructor)\<br>\<br>Units: 3-0-9\<br>\<br>* * *\<br>\<br>Introduces computational aspects of computer-aided design and manufacturing. Explores relevant methods in the context of additive manufacturing (e.g., 3D printing). Topics include computer graphics (geometry modeling, solid modeling, procedural modeling), physically-based simulation (kinematics, finite element method), 3D scanning/geometry processing, and an overview of 3D fabrication methods. Exposes students to the latest research in computational fabrication. Students taking the graduate version complete additional assignments.](http://student.mit.edu/catalog/search.cgi?search=6.4420)<br>[6.45906.805![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/spring.gif)\<br>**6.4590 Foundations of Information Policy**\<br>\<br>* * *\<br>\<br>![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/nooffer.gif)![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/under.gif) (![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/spring.gif))\<br>\<br>(Same subject as STS.085)\<br>\<br>Prereqs: Permission of instructor\<br>\<br>Units: 3-0-9\<br>\<br>* * *\<br>\<br>Studies the interaction of law, public policy, and technology in today's controversies over control of the Internet. Students use technical, legal, and rhetorical skills to analyze and participate in the evolution of global public policy frameworks. Explores lessons for the future of increasingly large-scale data analytics systems including AI-based technologies. Instruction on how to write persuasive technology policy pieces, refine oral policy presentation skills through role-playing simulations, and develop original responses to contemporary digital policy challenges provided. Topics include: history of Internet policy, the relationship between technical architecture and law, privacy, freedom of expression, platform regulation, privacy, intellectual property, digital surveillance, and international affairs. Students taking graduate version complete additional assignments. _Additional constraints:_

-

At least _two_ of your completed subjects
must be on the [CIM2](https://eecsis.mit.edu/degree_requirements.pcgi?program=6-P4#CIM2) list

-

At least _one_ of your completed subjects
must be on the [AI+D\_SERC](https://eecsis.mit.edu/degree_requirements.pcgi?program=6-P4#AI+D_SERC) list

-

At least _one_ of your completed subjects
must be on the [Data-centric](https://eecsis.mit.edu/degree_requirements.pcgi?program=6-P4#Data-centric) list

-

At least _one_ of your completed subjects
must be on the [Model-centric](https://eecsis.mit.edu/degree_requirements.pcgi?program=6-P4#Model-centric) list

-

At least _one_ of your completed subjects
must be on the [Decision-centric](https://eecsis.mit.edu/degree_requirements.pcgi?program=6-P4#Decision-centric) list

-

At least _one_ of your completed subjects
must be on the [Computation-centric](https://eecsis.mit.edu/degree_requirements.pcgi?program=6-P4#Computation-centric) list

-

At least _one_ of your completed subjects
must be on the [Human-centric](https://eecsis.mit.edu/degree_requirements.pcgi?program=6-P4#Human-centric) list

- At least _three_ of your subjects must from the same
[MEng Concentration](https://eecsis.mit.edu/degree_requirements.pcgi?program=6-P4#MEng_concentration)
area (see below).
At least _one_ of your concentration subjects must be on the
[AAGS](https://eecsis.mit.edu/degree_requirements.pcgi?program=6-P4#AAGS) list.

_Notes:_

- You must follow these requirements
if you entered MIT in Fall 2025 or later.

- Includes subjects as of Fall 2026
- Each completed subject can only be used to satisfy at most one _required_
subject but can be used to satisfy multiple _additional constraints_.
- ![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/fall.gif): only offered fall term

![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/spring.gif): only offered spring term

grey: not offered this academic year.
- If you choose a Math requirement as an elective, it must not have [essentially similar content](https://math.mit.edu/academics/undergrad/major/#similar) to the other subjects satisfying your 6-4 degree requirements.
- You are allowed to petition for one out-of-department AAGS that complements your MEng program. Submit your petition prior to taking the class. These petitions, like all departmental petitions, are reviewed on a case-by-case basis and are not guaranteed to be approved.

**MEng Concentration**
Three-subject concentration from one of the following areas. At least one
of the concentration subjects must be an AAGS (marked with an asterisk).

- _Applied Physics_

Quasistatic Maxwell's equations and the Lorentz force law. Studies of the quasistatic fields and their sources through solutions of Poisson's and Laplace's equations. Boundary conditions and multi-region boundary-value problems. Steady-state conduction, polarization, and magnetization. Charge conservation and relaxation, and magnetic induction and diffusion. Extension to moving materials. Electric and magnetic forces and force densities derived from energy, and stress tensors. Extensive use of engineering examples. Students taking graduate version complete additional assignments.](http://student.mit.edu/catalog/search.cgi?search=6.6210)<br>[6.62206.334![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/spring.gif)\*\<br>**6.6220 Power Electronics**\<br>\<br>* * *\<br>\<br>![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/grad.gif) (![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/spring.gif))\<br>\<br>Prereqs: 6.2040, 6.2080, 6.2220, or 6.2500\<br>\<br>Units: 3-0-9\<br>\<br>* * *\<br>\<br>The application of electronics to energy conversion and control. Lumped-parameter electromechanics. Power flow using Poynting's theorem, force estimation using the Maxwell stress tensor and Principle of virtual work. The third round, students conduct themselves as the class project on an imaging system of their choice. The undergraduate and graduate versions share lectures but have different recitations. Throughout the term, we also conduct optional "clinics" to even out background knowledge of linear algebra, optimization, and computational imaging-related programming best practices for students of diverse disciplinary backgrounds.](http://student.mit.edu/catalog/search.cgi?search=6.C67) |

- _Circuits_

| [6.20406.101![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/spring.gif)\<br>**6.2040 Analog Electronics Laboratory**\<br>\<br>* * *\<br>\<br>![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/under.gif) (![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/spring.gif))\<br>\<br>Prereqs: 6.2000\<br>\<br>Units: 2-9-1\<br>\<br>* * *\<br>\<br>Experimental laboratory explores the design, construction, and debugging of analog electronic circuits. Projects involve design, implementation, and presentation in an environment similar to that of industry engineering design teams. Instruction and practice in written and oral communication provided. - _Communications_

Lossless source coding; variable-length and block compression; Slepian-Wolf theorem; ergodic sources and Shannon-McMillan theorem. Hypothesis testing, large deviations and I-projection. Fundamental limits of block coding for noisy channels: capacity, dispersion, finite blocklength bounds. Coding with feedback. Joint source-channel problem. Rate-distortion theory, vector quantizers. Advanced topics include Gelfand-Pinsker problem, multiple access channels, broadcast channels (depending on available time).](http://student.mit.edu/catalog/search.cgi?search=6.7470) | [6.74806.260![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/fall.gif)\*\<br>**6.7480 Information Theory: From Coding to Learning**\<br>\<br>* * *\<br>\<br>![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/nooffer.gif)![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/grad.gif) (![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/fall.gif))\<br>\<br>Prereqs: 6.3700, 6.3800, or 18.05\<br>\<br>Units: 3-0-9\<br>\<br>* * *\<br>\<br>Introduces fundamentals of information theory and its applications to contemporary problems in statistics, machine learning, and computer science. A thorough study of information measures, including Fisher information, f-divergences, their convex duality, and variational characterizations. Covers information-theoretic treatment of inference, hypothesis testing and large deviations, universal compression, channel coding, lossy compression, and strong data-processing inequalities. Methods are applied to deriving PAC-Bayes bounds, GANs, and regret inequalities in machine learning, parametric and non-parametric estimation in statistics, communication complexity, and computation with noisy gates in computer science. - _Computer Systems_

- _Numerical Methods_

| [6.72006.2556.7200 not found in MIT subject catalog](http://student.mit.edu/catalog/search.cgi?search=6.7200)<br>[6.72016.2156.7201 not found in MIT subject catalog](http://student.mit.edu/catalog/search.cgi?search=6.7201)<br>[6.72106.251![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/fall.gif)\*\<br>**6.7210 Introduction to Mathematical Programming**\<br>\<br>* * *\<br>\<br>![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/grad.gif) (![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/fall.gif))\<br>\<br>(Same subject as 15.081)\<br>\<br>Prereqs: 18.06\<br>\<br>Units: 4-0-8\<br>\<br>* * *\<br>\<br>Introduction to linear optimization and its extensions emphasizing both methodology and the underlying mathematical structures and geometrical ideas. Covers classical theory of linear programming as well as some recent advances in the field. Topics: simplex method; duality theory; sensitivity analysis; network flow problems; decomposition; robust optimization; integer programming; interior point algorithms for linear programming; and introduction to combinatorial optimization and NP-completeness.](http://student.mit.edu/catalog/search.cgi?search=6.7210)<br>[6.72206.252![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/spring.gif)\*\<br>**6.7220 Nonlinear Optimization**\<br>\<br>* * *\<br>\<br>![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/grad.gif) (![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/spring.gif))\<br>\<br>(Same subject as 15.084)\<br>\<br>Prereqs: 18.06 and (18.100A, 18.100B, or 18.100Q)\<br>\<br>Units: 4-0-8\<br>\<br>* * *\<br>\<br>Unified analytical and computational approach to nonlinear optimization problems. Unconstrained optimization methods include gradient, conjugate direction, Newton, sub-gradient and first-order methods. Constrained optimization methods include feasible directions, projection, interior point methods, and Lagrange multiplier methods. Convex analysis, Lagrangian relaxation, nondifferentiable optimization, and applications in integer programming. Comprehensive treatment of optimality conditions and Lagrange multipliers. Geometric approach to duality theory. - _Theoretical CS_

**Subject Lists****AAGS**:
Approved advanced graduate subjects are graduate subjects that
build on foundational knowledge to develop advanced (and often
state-of-the-art) expertise in a field of interest.

**AI+D\_SERC**:
Social and Ethical Responsibilies of Computing

Case studies of working systems and readings from the current literature provide comparisons and contrasts. Possible topics include the implications of hierarchical designs (e.g., DNS) for scale; how layered models influence what parts of a network have the power to take certain actions; and the environmental impact of proof-of-work-based systems such as Bitcoin. Students taking graduate version complete additional assignments. | [6.31006.302\<br>**6.3100 Dynamical System Modeling and Control Design**\<br>\<br>* * *\<br>\<br>![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/under.gif) (![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/fall.gif),![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/spring.gif))\<br>\<br>Prereqs: GIR:PHY2 and (18.06 or 18.C06)\<br>\<br>Units: 4-4-4\<br>\<br>* * *\<br>\<br>A learn-by-design introduction to modeling and control of discrete- and continuous-time systems, from intuition-building analytical techniques to more computational and data-centric strategies. Concepts are introduced with lectures and online problems, and then mastered during weekly labs. In lab, students model, design, test, and explain systems and controllers involving sensors, actuators, and a microcontroller (e.g., optimizing thrust-driven positioners or stabilizing magnetic levitators). **EECS**:
All subjects that satisfy departmental undergraduate requirements in 6-1, 6-2, 6-3, 6-4, or 6-5 excluding subjects that are 6 units or less. Also see the **grad\_AUS**, **grad\_AI+D\_AUS**, and **grad\_II** lists.

| [18.03\<br>**18.03 Differential Equations**\<br>\<br>* * *\<br>\<br>![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/under.gif) (![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/fall.gif),![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/spring.gif))\<br>\<br>Prereqs/\[Coreqs\]: \[GIR:CAL2\]\<br>\<br>Units: 5-0-7\<br>\<br>* * *\<br>\<br>Study of differential equations, including modeling physical systems. Solution of first-order ODEs by analytical, graphical, and numerical methods. Linear ODEs with constant coefficients. Complex numbers and exponentials. Inhomogeneous equations: polynomial, sinusoidal, and exponential inputs. Oscillations, damping, resonance. Fourier series. Matrices, eigenvalues, eigenvectors, diagonalization. First order linear systems: normal modes, matrix exponentials, variation of parameters. Heat equation, wave equation. Nonlinear autonomous systems: critical point analysis, phase plane diagrams.](http://student.mit.edu/catalog/search.cgi?search=18.03)<br>[18.032![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/spring.gif)\<br>**18.032 Differential Equations**\<br>\<br>* * *\<br>\<br>![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/under.gif) (![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/spring.gif))\<br>\<br>Prereqs/\[Coreqs\]: \[GIR:CAL2\]\<br>\<br>Units: 5-0-7\<br>\<br>* * *\<br>\<br>Covers much of the same material as 18.03 with more emphasis on theory. The point of view is rigorous and results are proven. Local existence and uniqueness of solutions.](http://student.mit.edu/catalog/search.cgi?search=18.032)<br>[18.05![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/spring.gif)\<br>**18.05 Introduction to Probability and Statistics**\<br>\<br>* * *\<br>\<br>![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/under.gif) (![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/spring.gif))\<br>\<br>Prereqs: GIR:CAL2\<br>\<br>Units: 4-0-8\<br>\<br>* * *\<br>\<br>A unified introduction to probability, Bayesian inference, and frequentist statistics. Topics include: combinatorics, random variables, (joint) distributions, covariance, central limit theorem; Bayesian updating, odds, posterior prediction; significance tests, confidence intervals, bootstrapping, regression. Students also develop computational skills and statistical thinking by using R to simulate, analyze, and visualize data; and by exploring privacy, fairness, and causality in contemporary media and research. Flipped subject taught in a Technology Enabled Active Learning (TEAL) classroom to facilitate discussion, group problem solving, and coding studios with ample mentorship.](http://student.mit.edu/catalog/search.cgi?search=18.05)<br>[18.06![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/spring.gif)\<br>**18.06 Linear Algebra**\<br>\<br>* * *\<br>\<br>![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/under.gif) (![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/spring.gif))\<br>\<br>Prereqs: GIR:CAL2\<br>\<br>Units: 4-0-8\<br>\<br>* * *\<br>\<br>Basic subject on matrix theory and linear algebra, emphasizing topics useful in other disciplines, including systems of equations, vector spaces, determinants, eigenvalues, singular value decomposition, and positive definite matrices. Applications to least-squares approximations, stability of differential equations, networks, Fourier transforms, and Markov processes. Uses linear algebra software. Compared with 18.700, more emphasis on matrix algorithms and many applications.](http://student.mit.edu/catalog/search.cgi?search=18.06)<br>[18.404![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/fall.gif)\<br>**18.404 Theory of Computation**\<br>\<br>* * *\<br>\<br>![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/under.gif) (![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/fall.gif))\<br>\<br>Prereqs: 6.1200 or 18.200\<br>\<br>Units: 4-0-8\<br>\<br>* * *\<br>\<br>A more extensive and theoretical treatment of the material in 6.1400J/18.400J, emphasizing computability and computational complexity theory. Regular and context-free languages. Decidable and undecidable problems, reducibility, recursive function theory. **MATH**:
Any non-calculus subject that satisfies a [Math (course 18) SB requirement](https://math.mit.edu/academics/undergrad/major/) but does not have [essentially similar content](https://math.mit.edu/academics/undergrad/major/#similar) to the other subjects satisfying your SB degree requirements.

**MEng Restricted Electives**:

- At most one from [18.05![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/spring.gif)\\
**18.05 Introduction to Probability and Statistics**\\
\\
* * *\\
\\
![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/under.gif) (![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/spring.gif))\\
\\
Prereqs: GIR:CAL2\\
\\
Units: 4-0-8\\
\\
* * *\\
\\
A unified introduction to probability, Bayesian inference, and frequentist statistics. Topics include: combinatorics, random variables, (joint) distributions, covariance, central limit theorem; Bayesian updating, odds, posterior prediction; significance tests, confidence intervals, bootstrapping, regression. Students also develop computational skills and statistical thinking by using R to simulate, analyze, and visualize data; and by exploring privacy, fairness, and causality in contemporary media and research. Flipped subject taught in a Technology Enabled Active Learning (TEAL) classroom to facilitate discussion, group problem solving, and coding studios with ample mentorship.](http://student.mit.edu/catalog/search.cgi?search=18.05), [18.44018.440 not found in MIT subject catalog](http://student.mit.edu/catalog/search.cgi?search=18.440), [18.600\\
**18.600 Probability and Random Variables**\\
\\
* * *\\
\\
![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/under.gif) (![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/fall.gif),![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/spring.gif))\\
\\
Prereqs: GIR:CAL2\\
\\
Units: 4-0-8\\
\\
* * *\\
\\
Probability spaces, random variables, distribution functions. Binomial, geometric, hypergeometric, Poisson distributions. Uniform, exponential, normal, gamma and beta distributions. Conditional probability, Bayes theorem, joint distributions. Chebyshev inequality, law of large numbers, and central limit theorem. Credit cannot also be received for 6.041A or 6.041B.](http://student.mit.edu/catalog/search.cgi?search=18.600), [6.37006.041\\
**6.3700 Introduction to Probability**\\
\\
* * *\\
\\
![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/under.gif) (![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/fall.gif),![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/spring.gif))\\
\\
Prereqs: GIR:CAL2\\
\\
Units: 4-0-8\\
\\
* * *\\
\\
An introduction to probability theory, the modeling and analysis of probabilistic systems, and elements of statistical inference. Probabilistic models, conditional probability. Discrete and continuous random variables. Expectation and conditional expectation, and further topics about random variables. Limit Theorems. Bayesian estimation and hypothesis testing. Elements of classical statistical inference. Bernoulli and Poisson processes. Markov chains. Students taking graduate version complete additional assignments.](http://student.mit.edu/catalog/search.cgi?search=6.3700), [6.77006.436![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/fall.gif)\\
**6.7700 Fundamentals of Probability**\\
\\
* * *\\
\\
![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/grad.gif) (![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/fall.gif))\\
\\
(Same subject as 15.085)\\
\\
Prereqs: GIR:CAL2\\
\\
Units: 4-0-8\\
\\
* * *\\
\\
Introduction to probability theory. Probability spaces and measures. Discrete and continuous random variables. Conditioning and independence. Multivariate normal distribution. Abstract integration, expectation, and related convergence results. Moment generating and characteristic functions. Bernoulli and Poisson process. Finite-state Markov chains. Convergence notions and their relations. Limit theorems. Familiarity with elementary probability and real analysis is desirable.](http://student.mit.edu/catalog/search.cgi?search=6.7700)

**6.1200 Mathematics for Computer Science**\\
\\
* * *\\
\\

Matrices, eigenvalues, eigenvectors, diagonalization. First order linear systems: normal modes, matrix exponentials, variation of parameters. Heat equation, wave equation. Nonlinear autonomous systems: critical point analysis, phase plane diagrams.](http://student.mit.edu/catalog/search.cgi?search=18.03), [18.032![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/spring.gif)\\

**18.C06 Linear Algebra and Optimization**\\
\\
* * *\\
\\
![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/under.gif) (![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/fall.gif))\\
\\
(Same subject as 6.C06)\\
\\
Prereqs: GIR:CAL2\\
\\
Units: 5-0-7\\
\\
* * *\\
\\
Introductory course in linear algebra and optimization, assuming no prior exposure to linear algebra and starting from the basics, including vectors, matrices, eigenvalues, singular values, and least squares. Covers the basics in optimization including convex optimization, linear/quadratic programming, gradient descent, and regularization, building on insights from linear algebra. Explores a variety of applications in science and engineering, where the tools developed give powerful ways to understand complex systems and also extract structure from data.](http://student.mit.edu/catalog/search.cgi?search=18.C06), [6.C06![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/fall.gif)\\

- At most one from [18.650\\
**18.650 Fundamentals of Statistics**\\
\\
* * *\\
\\
![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/under.gif) (![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/fall.gif),![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/spring.gif))\\
\\
(Same subject as IDS.014)\\
\\
Prereqs: 6.3700 or 18.600\\
\\
Units: 4-0-8\\
\\
* * *\\
\\
A rapid introduction to the theoretical foundations of statistical methods that are useful in many applications. Covers a broad range of topics in a short amount of time with the goal of providing a rigorous and cohesive understanding of the modern statistical landscape. Mathematical language is used for intuition and basic derivations but not proofs. Main topics include: parametric estimation, confidence intervals, hypothesis testing, Bayesian inference, and linear and logistic regression. Additional topics may include: causal inference, nonparametric estimation, and classification.](http://student.mit.edu/catalog/search.cgi?search=18.650), [18.6501\\

A rapid introduction to the theoretical foundations of statistical methods that are useful in many applications. Covers a broad range of topics in a short amount of time with the goal of providing a rigorous and cohesive understanding of the modern statistical landscape. Mathematical language is used for intuition and basic derivations but not proofs. Main topics include: parametric estimation, confidence intervals, hypothesis testing, Bayesian inference, and linear and logistic regression. Additional topics may include: causal inference, nonparametric estimation, and classification. * * *\\

Covers fundamental concepts in continuous applied mathematics. Additional topics may include sonic booms, Mach cone, caustics, lattices, dispersion and group velocity. Uses MATLAB computing environment.](http://student.mit.edu/catalog/search.cgi?search=18.300), [18.330![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/spring.gif)\\

Focuses on traditional algebra topics that have found greatest application in science and engineering as well as in mathematics: group theory, emphasizing finite groups; ring theory, including ideals and unique factorization in polynomial and Euclidean rings; field theory, including properties and applications of finite fields. 18.700 and 18.703 together form a standard algebra sequence.](http://student.mit.edu/catalog/search.cgi?search=18.703), [18.781![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/spring.gif)\\

**8.044 Statistical Physics I**\\
\\
* * *\\
\\
![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/under.gif) (![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/spring.gif))\\
\\
Prereqs: 8.03 and 18.03\\
\\
Units: 5-0-7\\
\\
* * *\\
\\
Introduction to probability, statistical mechanics, and thermodynamics. Random variables, joint and conditional probability densities, and functions of a random variable. Concepts of macroscopic variables and thermodynamic equilibrium, fundamental assumption of statistical mechanics, microcanonical and canonical ensembles. First, second, and third laws of thermodynamics. Numerous examples illustrating a wide variety of physical phenomena such as magnetism, polyatomic gases, thermal radiation, electrons in solids, and noise in electronic devices. Concurrent enrollment in 8.04 is recommended.](http://student.mit.edu/catalog/search.cgi?search=8.044)

| [6.30006.003\<br>**6.3000 Signal Processing**\<br>\<br>* * *\<br>\<br>![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/under.gif) (![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/fall.gif),![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/spring.gif))\<br>\<br>Prereqs: 6.100A\<br>\<br>Units: 4-0-8\<br>\<br>* * *\<br>\<br>Fundamentals of signal processing, focusing on the use of Fourier methods to analyze and process signals such as sounds and images. Topics include Fourier series, Fourier transforms, the Discrete Fourier Transform, sampling, convolution, deconvolution, filtering, noise reduction, and compression. Applications draw broadly from areas of contemporary interest with emphasis on both analysis and design.](http://student.mit.edu/catalog/search.cgi?search=6.3000)<br>[6.31006.302\<br>**6.3100 Dynamical System Modeling and Control Design**\<br>\<br>* * *\<br>\<br>![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/under.gif) (![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/fall.gif),![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/spring.gif))\<br>\<br>Prereqs: GIR:PHY2 and (18.06 or 18.C06)\<br>\<br>Units: 4-4-4\<br>\<br>* * *\<br>\<br>A learn-by-design introduction to modeling and control of discrete- and continuous-time systems, from intuition-building analytical techniques to more computational and data-centric strategies. Topics include: linear difference/differential equations (natural frequencies, transfer functions); controller metrics (stability, tracking, disturbance rejection); analytical techniques (PID, root-loci, lead-lag, phase margin); computational strategies (state-space, eigen-placement, LQR); and data-centric approaches (state estimation, regression, and identification). Concepts are introduced with lectures and online problems, and then mastered during weekly labs. In lab, students model, design, test, and explain systems and controllers involving sensors, actuators, and a microcontroller (e.g., optimizing thrust-driven positioners or stabilizing magnetic levitators). Students taking graduate version complete additional problems and labs.](http://student.mit.edu/catalog/search.cgi?search=6.3100)<br>[6.41106.038![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/spring.gif)\<br>**6.4110 Representation, Inference, and Reasoning in AI**\<br>\<br>* * *\<br>\<br>![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/under.gif) (![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/spring.gif))\<br>\<br>Prereqs: 6.1010, 6.1210, and (6.3700, 6.3800, 18.05, or 18.600)\<br>\<br>Units: 3-0-9\<br>\<br>* * *\<br>\<br>An introduction to representations and algorithms for artificial intelligence. Topics covered include: constraint satisfaction in discrete and continuous problems, logical representation and inference, Monte Carlo tree search, probabilistic graphical models and inference, planning in discrete and continuous deterministic and probabilistic models including Markov decision processes (MDPs) and partially observed Markov decision processes (POMDPs).](http://student.mit.edu/catalog/search.cgi?search=6.4110)<br>[6.44006.837![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/fall.gif)\<br>**6.4400 Computer Graphics**\<br>\<br>* * *\<br>\<br>![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/under.gif) (![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/fall.gif))\<br>\<br>Prereqs: 6.1010 and (18.06 or 18.C06)\<br>\<br>Units: 3-0-9\<br>\<br>* * *\<br>\<br>Introduction to computer graphics algorithms, software and hardware. Topics include ray tracing, the graphics pipeline, transformations, texture mapping, shadows, sampling, global illumination, splines, animation and color.](http://student.mit.edu/catalog/search.cgi?search=6.4400)<br>[6.44206.807![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/fall.gif)\<br>**6.4420 Computational Design and Fabrication**\<br>\<br>* * *\<br>\<br>![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/under.gif) (![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/fall.gif))\<br>\<br>(Same subject as 2.0911)\<br>\<br>Prereqs: GIR:CAL2 and (6.1010 or permission of instructor)\<br>\<br>Units: 3-0-9\<br>\<br>* * *\<br>\<br>Introduces computational aspects of computer-aided design and manufacturing. Explores relevant methods in the context of additive manufacturing (e.g., 3D printing). Topics include computer graphics (geometry modeling, solid modeling, procedural modeling), physically-based simulation (kinematics, finite element method), 3D scanning/geometry processing, and an overview of 3D fabrication methods. Exposes students to the latest research in computational fabrication. Students taking the graduate version complete additional assignments.](http://student.mit.edu/catalog/search.cgi?search=6.4420) |

**grad\_AI+D\_AUS**:
Graduate subjects that satisfy the **AI+D\_AUS** or **EECS** requirements

| [6.3932![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/fall.gif)\<br>**6.3932 AI and Decision Making in Medicine: From Disease to Therapy**\<br>\<br>* * *\<br>\<br>![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/grad.gif) (![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/fall.gif))\<br>\<br>Prereqs: 6.100A and 6.C01\<br>\<br>Units: 3-0-9\<br>\<br>* * *\<br>\<br>Introduction to fundamental principles and applications of artificial intelligence (AI) in medicine and medical research. Introduces foundational concepts in machine learning as they pertain to clinical decision support systems, personalized medicine, and advanced computational methods for drug optimization and protein folding. Stresses the interplay of clinical reasoning, risk stratification, and the design of novel therapeutics. Problem sets integrate theoretical knowledge and practical applications based on concrete problems in both medical and pharmaceutical science. Students taking graduate version complete additional assignments.](http://student.mit.edu/catalog/search.cgi?search=6.3932)<br>[6.53406.853![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/spring.gif)\<br>**6.5340 Topics in Algorithmic Game Theory**\<br>\<br>* * *\<br>\<br>![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/nooffer.gif)![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/grad.gif) (![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/spring.gif))\<br>\<br>Prereqs: 6.1210 or 6.1220\<br>\<br>Units: 3-0-9\<br>\<br>* * *\<br>\<br>Presents research topics at the interface of computer science and game theory, with an emphasis on algorithms and computational complexity. Explores the types of game-theoretic tools that are applicable to computer systems, the loss in system performance due to the conflicts of interest of users and administrators, and the design of systems whose performance is robust with respect to conflicts of interest inside the system. Algorithmic focus is on algorithms for equilibria, the complexity of equilibria and fixed points, algorithmic tools in mechanism design, learning in games, and the price of anarchy.](http://student.mit.edu/catalog/search.cgi?search=6.5340)<br>[6.5940![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/fall.gif)\<br>**6.5940 TinyML and Efficient Deep Learning Computing**\<br>\<br>* * *\<br>\<br>![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/grad.gif) (![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/fall.gif))\<br>\<br>Prereqs: 6.1910 and 6.3900\<br>\<br>Units: 3-0-9\<br>\<br>* * *\<br>\<br>Introduces efficient deep learning computing techniques that enable powerful deep learning applications on resource-constrained devices. Topics include model compression, pruning, quantization, neural architecture search, distributed training, data/model parallellism, gradient compression, on-device fine-tuning. It also introduces application-specific acceleration techniques for video recognition, point cloud, and generative AI (diffusion model, LLM). Topics include finite and infinite groups (with an introduction to Lie algebras), various group representations (regular, reducible, and irreducible), tensor products and decompositions, Fourier analysis and convolutions, statistics and sampling of representation vector spaces, and symmetry-breaking mechanisms. Previous knowledge of group theory is not required but is beneficial.](http://student.mit.edu/catalog/search.cgi?search=6.7970)<br>[6.7980![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/fall.gif)\<br>**6.7980 Topics in Multiagent Learning**\<br>\<br>* * *\<br>\<br>![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/grad.gif) (![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/fall.gif))\<br>\<br>Prereqs: 6.1200, 6.1220, or 15.C571\<br>\<br>Units: 3-0-9\<br>\<br>* * *\<br>\<br>Examines the foundations of multi-agent systems from a combined game-theoretic, optimization, and learning-theoretic perspective. Studies modern settings deviating from standard models wherein strategies and utilities are represented using deep neural networks. **grad\_AUS2**:

Unfolding and folding three-dimensional polyhedra: edge unfolding, vertex unfolding, gluings, Alexandrov's Theorem, hinged dissections.](http://student.mit.edu/catalog/search.cgi?search=6.5310)<br>[6.53206.850![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/spring.gif)\<br>**6.5320 Geometric Computing**\<br>\<br>* * *\<br>\<br>![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/nooffer.gif)![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/grad.gif) (![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/spring.gif))\<br>\<br>Prereqs: 6.1220\<br>\<br>Units: 3-0-9\<br>\<br>* * *\<br>\<br>Introduction to the design and analysis of algorithms for geometric problems, in low- and high-dimensional spaces. Algorithms: convex hulls, polygon triangulation, Delaunay triangulation, motion planning, pattern matching. Geometric data structures: point location, Voronoi diagrams, Binary Space Partitions. Geometric problems in higher dimensions: linear programming, closest pair problems. High-dimensional nearest neighbor search and low-distortion embeddings between metric spaces. Geometric optimization.](http://student.mit.edu/catalog/search.cgi?search=6.5320)<br>[6.53406.853![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/spring.gif)\<br>**6.5340 Topics in Algorithmic Game Theory**\<br>\<br>* * *\<br>\<br>![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/nooffer.gif)![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/grad.gif) (![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/spring.gif))\<br>\<br>Prereqs: 6.1210 or 6.1220\<br>\<br>Units: 3-0-9\<br>\<br>* * *\<br>\<br>Presents research topics at the interface of computer science and game theory, with an emphasis on algorithms and computational complexity. **grad\_II**:
Graduate subjects that satisfy the **II** additional constraint or **EECS** requirement

| [6.1852![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/fall.gif)\<br>**6.1852 Computer Systems and Society**\<br>\<br>* * *\<br>\<br>![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/grad.gif) (![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/fall.gif))\<br>\<br>Prereqs: 6.1800\<br>\<br>Units: 3-0-9\<br>\<br>* * *\<br>\<br>Explores the impact of computer systems on individual humans, society, and the environment. Studies modern settings deviating from standard models wherein strategies and utilities are represented using deep neural networks. Presents equilibrium concepts, equilibrium computation methods, and equilibrium learning methods. Studies the complexity of solution concepts. Discusses applications in machine learning, including multi-agent reinforcement learning, adversarial learning, AI-AI interactions, and strategic decision-making systems underlying game-playing agents in domains such as Go, poker, Diplomacy, and Stratego.](http://student.mit.edu/catalog/search.cgi?search=6.7980) | [6.8130![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/fall.gif)\<br>**6.8130 Embodied Intelligence**\<br>\<br>* * *\<br>\<br>![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/nooffer.gif)![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/grad.gif) (![](https://eecsis.mit.edu/images/fall.gif))\<br>\<br>Prereqs: (6.1210 or 6.1220) and (6.3900 or 6.7900)\<br>\<br>Units: 3-0-9\<br>\<br>* * *\<br>\<br>Explores the problem of designing a general-purpose intelligent robot, addressing challenges in robot design, such as long-horizon decision making, continuous action spaces, partial observability, and nonstationary and adversarial environments.
